B004FGMDOQ|3.0|I can't deny that Miss Peregrine's Home for Peculiar Children boasts a really fascinating hook; by incorporating strange and unusual vintage photographs into the book and the story, first-time author Ransom Riggs creates an odd blending of fantasy and \\"reality\\" of sorts. And there's no denying that the pictures are compellingly odd and even somewhat unsettling, even without the context of the story around them. But in the end, without those photographs, Peregrine is a fairly generic YA adventure story, one that does a lot less with its premise than you would hope. The book is strongest in its earliest chapters, as the reader attempts to investigate the mystery of these photos along with the protagonist, and questions whether they're plunging into a land of mystery or chasing a made-up story spun by a master raconteur. Unfortunately, the answer to all of that ends up being a lot less interesting than the mystery, especially since it involves just another variation on an X-Men style team of unusual powers and odd children. The book attempts to overcome this by developing a bit of a mythology as it goes along, but it's never a particularly compelling or original one, and by the end it devolves into something I never expected: a book that's really pretty generic, complete with the now-obligatory setup for a trilogy of tales. I had high hopes for Peregrine, and in the early going I thought I'd found a compelling look at the way we use mysteries and myths to make sense of the horrors of our own world, or a fascinating peek into a world of oddities and misfits. Instead, it's just another bland YA adventure book, one that doesn't have much to recommend it beyond an interesting hook that never functions as more than a gimmick.

B005LCECU6|5.0|John Rutsey (RIP) was a very good drummer. When I first picked up the second Rush album, \\"Fly by Night,\\" I noticed a couple things. The album actually had cover art, an owl looking menacing graced the cover. My first thought was that this meant the band had moved to a softer direction, probably on company orders. On the back cover, I discovered that Rutsey had been replaced by some guy named Neil Peart. My thinking went something like this...commercially appealing front cover, early personnel changes, they dumped a very good drummer for an unknown, sophmore slump, this album is going to suck but I'll give it a shot. I put it on as background music to study calculus. I didn't even make it to my chair before I recognized how amazing this new drummer was. I turned my chair around to actually listen to the first track, \\"Anthem.\\" Outstanding...but they always put their best song first. Surely they can't keep it up. By the conclusion of \\"By-Tor and the Snow Dog\\" on the first side, I was a beleiver. Neil Peart jumped ahead of Keith Moon as my favorite drummer. But how could this guy develop such a powerful and truly unique style of drumming and materialize out of nowhere? Can Peart drum this well live or is he just a quirk of the studio?<br /><br />Well, he could drum live and this CD reflects that. I'm wondering how it must have felt to see Rush when this CD was recorded. Concert attendees were familiar with their hit, \\"Working Man,\\" and maybe even the rest of the first album. Behind the drumkit and not near the stage, many folks probably thought it was Rutsey. Can you imagine seeing Neil Peart during an early performance not knowing what a legend he would become?<br /><br />This CD is a magical performance, especially if you imagine seeing the show. For a bootleg, the recording is superb. Heck, I've heard many official releases that didn't sound this good. As a document of early Rush, this is an amazing find. I'd say it's a must even for the casual Rush fan.

You can hear it lyrically, Justin just hasn't lived and experienced life enough to pull these songs off convincingly. Because of this the songs are dead boring to listen to and Justin sounds dead bored singing them.<br /><br />The majority (not all) of the rest of the songs on the album are syrupy pop ballads whose sole purpose is to make girls under the age of 18 swoon and mediocre filler songs. While some people may be partial to one or two of the rest of these songs, for most listeners there is nothing here to write home about.<br /><br />My biggest issue is that this album is a mixed bag. Now that can be a good thing if it's done correctly but in Believe's case it's not. I could just picture about 20 different people pulling Justin in about 20 different directions. This album seems to be a disorganized mix of pop, R&B, & rap. While I understand Justin is young and still trying to find himself as an artist there aren't any songs that serve as a middle ground transition song between his switches (if that makes sense). If you listen to the album all the way though you'll get a better idea of what I mean. It seems to jump from a pop sound to an R&B and then back to pop in a \\"that just came outta left field kinda way.\\" Musically, the songs are ordered awkwardly and in general are just not organized into a smooth and natural transition that one comes to expect from a seasoned artist.<br /><br />Would I recommend this album to you? No, not unless you are a die hard Bieber fan or have listened to the album somewhere else before you purchase it. I think the majority of people will do what I plan on doing which is buy 2 or 3 of the top songs on the album and save the rest of your hard earned cash for something that is a more of a complete package. Is this album horrible? No, of course not. Justin has a lot of potential and over time he will hopefully develop into a well respected and seasoned adult artist. Unfortunately, his first attempt is mainly a flop. This is mostly because he is just trying way too hard to be this new and improved adult Bieber. You don't jump from one side of the ocean to the other. You need to sail across it. Bieber tried to leap over the ocean, but fell into it instead. He's still afloat and hasn't sunk yet. He needs to swim back to shore, slow down, and sail across. The journey to becoming a man isn't something that happens overnight. Nor is the journey to becoming a great artist.

1439153663|4.0|In her novel, \\"The Kitchen House,\\" Kathleen Grissom interjects much heart into the people interconnections between orphaned Lavinia and her adopted family of black slaves, even if some of its players rely upon cartoon-ish stereotypes and the plot revolves around a progression of grim realities that are so dire they crush all hope and blight even the reader with a tsunami's brute force and determined inundation that feels sour and overdone. The audience's despair is punctuated by the fact that the historical point in time doesn't always reflect the early 19th century setting or its early New Nation flavor.  In fact, the voices of the two women who narrate \\"The Kitchen House,\\" know little about the world outside their Virginia tobacco plantation and quite frankly, I doubt they care very much.  Perhaps in  terms of the actual events of the story, whose universe incorporates a small microcosm outside the larger world, this is normal and natural--recently during Hurricane Isaac, this reviewer lost power and satellite television--if it weren't for the car radio, there would have been no exposure to the goings-on outside this small sphere of influence.  More importantly, personal troubles, in a way, insulate and anesthetize one from the larger catastrophes of the bigger environment. Nevertheless, Grissom may concentrate on her crafted mini-planet as her women live it, but the multitude of tragedies weighs the work down and spirals it into the realm of disbelief.<br /><br />Grissom crafting of a post-Revolutionary war atmosphere suffers from the needs of the story.  I greatly doubt that any immigrant Irish girl who has lost her parents and is set to work in the kitchen of a plantation would ever be able to consort with the likes of the plantation owner's wife's family as she grows into young adulthood.  Although this contrivance suits the storyline well, from a realistic standpoint, it falls short and suspends belief.  Lavinia's eventual blossoming into the status of \\"lady\\" after scrubbing out the kitchen sink seems far-fetched indeed.<br /><br />Add to that the prescience of any reader already familiar with the usual dramatic slavery themes.  As soon as predictable and formulaic \\"Southern Plantation Event A\\" occurs (e.g. an unjustly treated slave breaks curfew), the savvy reader immediately anticipates B: burning crosses, C: whippings, D: lynchings, E: sell-offs or the separation of families and F: all sorts of other inhuman cruelty just because it is what it is. Grissom doesn't let up on this diabolical aspect of slave life or the stupidity of a system that sadly and freely discounts the worth of human life.  There are so many `uh-oh\\" and \\"oh-no\\" moments designed to depict the helplessness of the Papa George/Mama Mae family that the reader quickly relinquishes any possibility of any sort of a happy ending where the protagonists do more than still breath on the last page. As Grissom jam-packs as many of slavery's defects and their corresponding effects on her characters' lives as she possibly can, she ratchets up the terror, disappointment and misery to a point where the reader will roll his/her eyes in deference and beg for mercy--or at least a shorter termed horsewhipping. (Just kidding!)<br /><br />She relies on characters like the arch-villain Rankin, who puts the two into two-dimensional--a drunken scoundrel who uses the slaves for quick relief and whose agenda is only to keep the blacks in line with the same relentless anger of the archetypical Simon Legree of [[ASIN:0486440281 Uncle Tom's Cabin (Dover Thrift Editions)]].  Her Captain Pike--the owner of the plantation can accrue a fortune, run the plantation and his ships, but cannot seem to get his house in order or see that his son needs protection. Mr. Waters is the pedophile tutor responsible for upsetting the otherwise idyllic lives of the Twelve Oaks' children and Marshall, Captain Pike's hapless son who epitomizes the deteriorating standard has no solid purpose other than to run his family's holdings into the ground.  Because her whites (with the exception of church-going Will Stevens) support the system of slavery, Grissom portrays them as weak, corrupt, enraged, power-crazed and ultimately doomed.<br /><br />On the other hand, all her black characters seem outwardly benignly subservient.  However, when it comes to taking care of business, where the whites fail--doping themselves with either laudanum, liqueur or an unconscious acquiescence to \\"the way things are\\" whenever times get rough, the blacks reach down into steel cores, maneuvering events and people to ensure a troubled yet familiar status quo.<br /><br />It is in this respect that Grissom achieves a narrative that although pockmarked with one tragedy after another, still compels the reader to discover what does eventually happen.  She endows the first portion of her story, when Lavinia is a child with much more detail.  With a sure command of her characters' interrelationships, she reveals the plantation's ugly underbelly and the ensuing sorrow, rage and frustration that such an insular system brings to all effected.<br /><br />Of the two narrators, Belle seems to have more authenticity.  Her comments regarding her love of Ben and her wary relationship with Lucy are fraught with good humor and sting with the bittersweetness of making due.  As the story draws to a close, her voice sings out with more strength; her monologues lengthen as her basic nobility reaffirms itself.<br /><br />Unfortunately, Grissom takes an opposite tack with her other main character and narrator, Lavinia.  Her actions in the final third of the novel seem weak and unsubstantiated.  As a child, Lavinia shows understanding and perseverance; she stands tall for Marshall when he needs her and mothers little Sukey without blinking an eye.  Her subsequent weakness and penchant for drugs seems only a deus ex machina that ensures the safe passage of the remaining players in the denouement after the climatic fire scene.<br /><br />From a wider scope, Grissom underlines the limitations of each of her character categories.  Miss Martha, the Captain's wife is so bored and warped by her ill-conceived idea regarding her husband's relationship with Belle that she fizzles away her precious time by dosing herself with opiates.  The Captain knows what he must and should do yet, he falters indecisively when it comes to insuring Belle a good future.  His failure to recognize Marshall's tormentor or the malicious cruelty of Rankin pigeonholes him as a product of his own need to keep his plantation afloat at the expense of others.  Ben, Mama Mae, Belle, Lucy, Uncle, Papa George, Ida, Beattie and Fanny work around their inferior status, finding small meager happiness by whatever means works, being fully aware that joy and security is an ephemeral variable. Lavinia equates contentment with the warmth of her iconoclastic memories.  When reality doesn't facilitate her expectations, she, too, falls short--jumping to immature conclusions, deciding that she can fix the unfixable and knuckling under when reality finally hits.  Marshall fritters away his fortune for the sake of fostering anger ignited and fueled by the maliciousness of others.  Grissom seems to be commenting on the inability of people in general to find contentment even in an idyllic place.  For the bored Miss Marthas of the world, there is laudanum to dull the pain of ineptitude and ennui.  For the idealistic that crash after reality hits, drugs exist to numb the pain.  For the ill-equipped successors of family businesses there is violent sex with the slaves and brain-quieting alcohol.  In the character of Meg, Grissom writes in the only personage that seems at rest--she has her botany and unconventionally refuses to fall in with the rest who conform through marriage and ultimate subservience.  The others have little control, distorting the very concept of what it means to be free or owned.<br /><br />I listened to the audio book version of this novel performed wonderfully by Orlagh Cassidy (Lavinia) and Bahni Turpin (Belle). Orlagh as Lavinia speaks with a brogue as a child and lightens this as she grows older.  Bahni Turpin endows local color and personality to the character of Belle.  All in all, the story in this format kept me well entertained.<br /><br />Bottom line?  If you enjoy pre-Civil War stories set on a Southern plantation and are prepared for the cruelty rained down upon the slaves by unjust overseers and clueless masters, you might enjoy Kathleen Grissom's \\"The Kitchen House.\\"  Be prepared for some situations that will suspend your belief.  Be forewarned that a \\"happy ending\\" for this tale just means that most of the characters are still alive by the final page.  Grissom may be setting up to write a sequel, which if I can listen on audio while exercising or driving, why not?  Recommended with some caveats.<br />Diana Faillace Von Behren<br />\\"reneofc\\"c fire scene.<br /><br />From a wider scope, Grissom underlines the limitations of each of her character categories.  Miss Martha, the Captain's wife is so bored and warped by her ill-conceived idea regarding her husband's relationship with Belle that she fizzles away her precious time by dosing herself with opiates.  The Captain knows what he must and should do yet, he falters indecisively when it comes to insuring Belle a good future.  His failure to recognize Marshall's tormentor or the malicious cruelty of Rankin pigeonholes him as a product of his own need to keep his plantation afloat at the expense of others.  Ben, Mama Mae, Belle, Lucy, Uncle, Papa George, Ida, Beattie and Fanny work around their inferior status, finding small meager happiness by whatever means works, being fully aware that joy and security is an ephemeral variable. Lavinia equates contentment with the warmth of her iconoclastic memories.  When reality doesn't facilitate her expectations, she, too, falls short--jumping to immature conclusions, deciding that she can fix the unfixable and knuckling under when reality finally hits.  Marshall fritters away his fortune for the sake of fostering anger ignited and fueled by the maliciousness of others.  Grissom seems to be commenting on the inability of people in general to find contentment even in an idyllic place.  For the bored Miss Marthas of the world, there is laudanum to dull the pain of ineptitude and ennui.  For the idealistic that crash after reality hits, drugs exist to numb the pain.  For the ill-equipped successors of family businesses there is violent sex with the slaves and brain-quieting alcohol.  In the character of Meg, Grissom writes in the only personage that seems at rest--she has her botany and unconventionally refuses to fall in with the rest who conform through marriage and ultimate subservience.  The others have little control, distorting the very concept of what it means to be free or owned.<br /><br />I listened to the audio book version of this novel performed wonderfully by Orlagh Cassidy (Lavinia) and Bahni Turpin (Belle). Orlagh as Lavinia speaks with a brogue as a child and lightens this as she grows older.  Bahni Turpin endows local color and personality to the character of Belle.  All in all, the story in this format kept me well entertained.<br /><br />Bottom line?  If you enjoy pre-Civil War stories set on a Southern plantation and are prepared for the cruelty rained down upon the slaves by unjust overseers and clueless masters, you might enjoy Kathleen Grissom's \\"The Kitchen House.\\"  Be prepared for some situations that will suspend your belief.  Be forewarned that a \\"happy ending\\" for this tale just means that most of the characters are still alive by the final page.  Grissom may be setting up to write a sequel, which if I can listen on audio while exercising or driving, why not?  Recommended with some caveats.<br />Diana Faillace Von Behren<br />\\"reneofc\\"

0307577589|4.0|The novel's main plot is fairly straight forward: a recently shamed political/ financial reporter (Mikael Blomkvist) is hired by the elderly head of the Vanger family (a billionaire industrialist family) to investigate the disappearance and possible murder of his niece 36 years ago. The circumstances surrounding the disappearance is presented as a \\"locked room\\" mystery - meaning that the environment in which the incident happened was closed to the outside world, limiting the suspects to only a few people.<br /><br />Surrounding main plot line is another one concerning the novel's namesake, Lisbeth Salanders. This gal is a social misfit who works as a security consult / private investigator while hiding a life of troubles. Larsson starts off by introducing and developing both players (Mikael and Lisbeth) and plot lines separately before intertwining them into one overarching story.<br /><br />Not wanting to give away too much details, I am going to shift gears here and briefly mention some of the more impactful concepts Larsson deals with in the novel. Earlier I mention that the book deals with acts of violence against women, and that is certainly true. A lot of the material in the book deals with this topic and how people deal with this violence - both men and women.<br /><br />One of the characters, for example, deals with this violence by taking revenge in their hands and dealing out violence in return. Part of this eye-for-an-eye mentally mostly likely comes from Larsson's own childhood when he witnessed a gang rape of a young lady and was powerless to do anything. As such, I can see why some of his characters seek this revenge - both for themselves and for others. Yet, on the other hand, I, as a follower of Jesus, can't help but remember the words of my King who says that revenge is His (Romans 12:19).  Violence begets violence; lack of trust begets lack of trust - a fact that in some ways is bore out in this book (abet subtly as this is not a theme of the book...it more of an observation on the essence of the characters)<br /><br />Another theme that Larsson does explore is one of ethics. What do you do if you are confronted with two evils and not good solutions? How do you choose a way forward in life when in doing so will cause pain at some level? Yet, a decision as to be made and, yes, the lack of a decision is still a decision....<br /><br />Larsson also does a great job at exploring the lives of those who are living under society's radar. Those folks whom society has deemed unfit or worse... How do they live? What are their motivations, pains, happiness, etc.?<br /><br />In a lot of ways this last theme was one of the hardest for me as my heart broke for those who had no one to trust...they were quite literally all alone in a sea of humanity. They don't even trust the official authorities that we in middle class USA take for granted (i.e. the police, justice system, etc.)...  Folks who are supposed to take care of them don't; instead they abuse their power and rape them (literally and figuratively) for their own pleasure.<br /><br />In ending, \\"The Girl With The Dragon Tattoo\\" by Stieg Larsson is a powerful book that keeps you engaged and has an amazing twist at the end. However, it is also a very intense and emotional book that may not be for everyone. In fact some of you will most likely throw the book across the table as Larsson does not retreat for descriptive vocabulary at the pains experienced by women every second across the planet.<br /><br />Yeah...it is not a `light' read. But it does bring up things, real life things, that are worth thinking about...it more of an observation on the essence of the characters)<br /><br />Another theme that Larsson does explore is one of ethics. Those folks whom society has deemed unfit or worse... How do they live? What are their motivations, pains, happiness, etc.?<br /><br />In a lot of ways this last theme was one of the hardest for me as my heart broke for those who had no one to trust...they were quite literally all alone in a sea of humanity. Folks who are supposed to take care of them don't; instead they abuse their power and rape them (literally and figuratively) for their own pleasure.<br /><br />In ending, \\"The Girl With The Dragon Tattoo\\" by Stieg Larsson is a powerful book that keeps you engaged and has an amazing twist at the end. A great thing about this is that Miller focuses a lot more on the importance of the story. I love Dark Knight Returns but he often focuses on adding extra grit and portrays Batman as a sadist and more of an anti-Hero than a hero. This is great for DKR, as an Elseworlds story and answering the question: What if Batman retired? In Year One however, we want to see how a young boy born into enormous wealth becomes a man of virtue seeking to bring Justice to a corrupt and crime addled metropolis (not to be confused with Metropolis). Miller gives us just that. People knock the art style, and some call it dated. Well first this was done 24 years ago. Second, this simple art is just what we need. Batman is not supposed to have some fancy well-detailed costume. He doesn't even know he'll become Batman precisely until the end of the first chapter. Billionaire yes, but these are his sort of humble beginnings, as you can see when reading this book, he also doesn't happen to take down 20 men without taking a scratch. He engages in full on brawls and sometimes he barely makes it out. It's precisely that dynamic that sets this book apart and makes it great.

From 2006, for example, the US Government was and no doubt still is `the world's largest consumer of goods and services; the aggregate spending of the 50 States not far behind.'<br /><br />If one could simplify this ongoing private-public sector relationship, it would be to point out that the US Government has a continuing need for the research, the technologies and the products and services initiated and produced by the private sector. The authors cite the DoD (Department of Defense), the Department of Energy (DoE) and NASA as examples of research-intensive government agencies which in their words, `have started to rely on the private sector to provide the latest and greatest available technologies.'<br /><br />All this of course impacts on procurement process in general and on government contracts in particular.  The book, therefore, as the publishers have put it, has embarked on `a comprehensive appraisal of the intellectual property implications of state and federal procurement programs in the United States'<br /><br />Now in a new second edition from OUP, the book provides a wealth of updated examination and analysis of the rules and strategies for handling government use of private sector IP rights - and sometimes their misuse. New developments that have emerged over the last three years (since the first edition was published) are included.<br /><br />Yes, the book is American, but the issues and problems it raises in the arena of protecting and enforcing clients' intellectual property rights are applicable globally, particularly to mixed economies like that of the UK.<br /><br />More or less half the book concentrates on issues relating to federal procurement practices and the like.  The latter half, in a lengthy and final chapter, deals individually with each on the 50 states in turn, from Alabama and Alaska to Virginia, Washington and the District of Columbia, covering such areas as intellectual property laws, procurement laws, technology transfer and sovereign immunity waivers.<br /><br />With some 20 pages of cases and a detailed index at the back, this copiously footnoted work of scholarship should be required reading for American IP lawyers, not to mention UK practitioners.<br /><br />And it is for all advisers requiring not only solid information and informed commentary, but a transatlantic perspective on those intellectual property issues which straddle the public and private sectors. He's a can-do guy who thinks he can figure a way to make it on his own, in spite of the fact that he and his family have lived the Army life, until now. There's a palpable distrust of people outside their family and an anti-social feeling throughout the book.<br /><br />The Parsons could be anybody's quiet next door neighbor, perhaps a little odd, but mostly average folks that keep to themselves. Mid-life pressures seem to have arisen for a husband in a predicament of finding a new career and his submissive wife, Neeva, a college-educated Jewish girl lost on the prairie. She longs for what could have been, if she didn't get pregnant and succumb to Bev's charms. Dell and Berner are the good product of their relationship but end up becoming castaways. Other than their introversion, their oddities are not particularly striking for a couple of adolescents.<br /><br />Ford will probably be remembered for the exceptional opening two sentences of the book that foretells the two main events that happen to Dell. This telegraphing what is to happen becomes an effective device used on other pages too.<br /><br />  \\"First, let me tell you about the robbery our parents committed. Then about the murders, which happened later.\\"<br /><br />The failed bank robbery tears apart the family and the murder afterwards actually results in bringing Dell back to normality - albeit in a different country. The book contains an unforgettable tapestry of characters and vivid atmosphere woven into a morality tale.<br /><br />The crimes his parents commit cause him to escape from the juvenile authorities to Canada.  His exile is arranged for him as he becomes a reluctant illegal immigrant refugee in a wild country with strange characters. Canada is to become the final destination for Dell. It's an accepting place where he can finally set roots even though its only 125 miles away from his last home in Great Falls, Montana.<br /><br />There's a theme that resonates throughout about what happens when we \\"cross the line\\". There are times we find ourselves at a precipice. Do we jump? Do we cross? Do we cheat? Do we steal? Would we kill? It's easy to say \\"I'd never do that!\\" But, Ford's point is there's a time when the right brew of people and circumstances allow the improbable to happen, despite possible tragic consequences. Once we cross that line we are irretrievably changed. There's no going back. Wisely, Ford does not spend much time rationalizing all this but leaves the reader to ponder on their own.<br /><br />In the end, Neeva, by all accounts is a loving mother. She leaves behind her writings from prison which try to make sense of her fate. She seems to absorb her fate and blame in titling her notebook writings, \\"The Chronicle of a Meek Person\\". She says in it \\"\\"You are only good if you can do bad and decide not to.\\" It begs the question as to whether she is was responsible for her actions or a victim of her limits - something she just didn't have the strength to overcome. Another deep philosophical question for the reader toponder.<br /><br />There's a pull to believe that the forces of nature and circumstance are controlling one's destiny, especially when a catastrophe befalls us.  We can't undo what's been done, even when it involves family. It's basically an optimistic viewpoint.  Even if you are a victim of circumstances, or even bad decisions, you still have a life to live and create for yourself. \\"You have a life and you need to fill it.\\"<br /><br />Dell tells of how he filled his life. His father, Bev, tried to fill his life with a delusional dream but filled his in prison. Neela fills hers as a prisoner of her personal life and then of the state, until she could not take it anymore. And Berner, never learns how to fill hers.<br /><br />There's a sense of Fitzgerald, Conrad and Salinger in the way this story is written and told by the narrator. Yet, mostly it's a unique conversation coming from a 60+ year old English teacher who is mostly speaking as a 15 year old. Perhaps Dell grew to be like his mother in the end. His book is a chronicle of events, much like she probably wrote as therapy in prison. It's straightforward, full of personal wisdom and, most of all, tries to make sense of a life lived but not chosen. The difference is that Dell could accept the world as it was, be was always willing to give up something and move on.ponder.<br /><br />There's a pull to believe that the forces of nature and circumstance are controlling one's destiny, especially when a catastrophe befalls us. 0525953728|4.0|I enjoyed this book. It's a collection of stories in rough sequential order from one of the most elite operators in the business, culminating with the Bin Laden raid.<br /><br />The narrative is very first-person, in the sense that you're rarely, if ever treated to a big-picture overview. It's more about how the author felt during his experiences. The weight of his load up a hill, the rising panic when his helicopter starts to falter. There's no flowery prose, just earnest descriptions that humanize a group of individuals more often portrayed as the progeny of Rambo and Robocop. They get tired. They miss. They're not immune to the allure of creature comforts (to the extent a hammock in the cargo bay might be considered one).<br /><br />And they have families, though these are often terribly shorted. Working at this level requires a commitment to train mind and body almost constantly. Everything else, the author says, takes a distant second. But this is not all self-sacrifice. The dirty secret of the SEAL community, he continues, is that they really enjoy their work.<br /><br />All of this is exciting to read. This book is very much a page-turner; I started and finished it on the same day. It's also a bit short. Most books of this format interweave action with history, alternating between adrenaline and perspective. This author doesn't. Save for a short aside on SEAL history, it's almost all ride. Or put another way, all wheat, no chaff.<br /><br />As with another reviewer, I was surprised just how much went wrong on the Bin Laden excursion, even with the most conscientious preparation. The SEAL modus operandi is to maximize unfairness. The ideal raid would catch their targets sleeping in underpants. Things get very precarious very quickly when efforts at surprise go awry. And yet, here, not one of the 20-odd boots on the ground was seriously injured. The most significant negative consequence was the incomplete destruction of a tail rotor. You really can't ask for a more sterling outcome.<br /><br />There are some political comments toward the end that some may take issue with, but frankly, for what these men contribute, they're entitled to whatever views they like. There's no grandstanding here. Nor is there overly revealing or otherwise untoward information. My feeling at the end was just relief that the special forces community is on our side. This book will almost certainly become a recruiting tool, not unlike the one that brought the author to the SEALs in the first place.more sterling outcome.<br /><br />There are some political comments toward the end that some may take issue with, but frankly, for what these men contribute, they're entitled to whatever views they like. Tough, antisocial, brilliant, devious, manipulative, these are all adjectives that could be used to describe her. When Stieg Larsson is writing about her, the book is exciting. When she is absent and Larsson is doing the back story it is boring. The opening is intense; Lisbeth is in the emergency room of a hospital having been shot in the head. The opening tenth of the book is about her. Will she survive and is there brain damage? Will she still possess her photographic memory? Then from this exciting beginning until the reader reaches beyond the two thirds point of the book Larsson builds his back story. It involves criminal activities of a secret group within the Swedish Intelligence Agency. They are intertwined with Lisbeth Salander but the pace is plodding. This subplot does nothing to advance the story line and is inconsequential. Blomkvist is the journalist who is trying to help and defend Lisbeth Salander. I almost quit reading the book, but plowed my way through this section. The last thirty percent becomes exciting again since all the disparate threads are pulled together and there is intense action. If this book could be tightened and shortened, it would be an excellent read, however, as it is, it takes some perseverance to finish.

In his century of life, his pursuit of vodka and a good meal took him from Sweden, across Europe, Asia, and the U.S.  Along the way, he dined with presidents and other key figures, crossed the Himalayas on foot, and aided in the development of the atomic bomb.<br /><br />Allan's simple, common-sense approach to life and Jonasson's wry observations make the book. B004W2UBYW|5.0|When I first heard about Walter Isaccson's effort to document Steve Jobs life in a biography I knew this was going to be on my must read list.  So much has been written about Jobs that I really looked forward to what I hoped would be a very dispassionate look at the man and the legend.  The writer did so successfully with his biography of Ben Franklin.  Similarly, 'Steve Jobs' does not disappoint.  This book receives here one of my rare 5-star reviews.<br /><br />The early influences of Jobs life - from his adoption to the role his parents played in nurturing a child they felt was special - are well sourced and presented.  There clearly exists a thread that is woven into the principal's fabric of being (self) that if pulled would seemingly unravel so much of what held all the pieces of a disparate personality together.<br /><br />As I read the book over a period of a couple of weeks I was traveling on business listening on my i-Pod, talking and surfing the web on my i-Phone while surrounded by so many others of my 'species' (Jobs wording) similarly engaged with myriad Apple products. This is the essential motivation of Jobs - making life more engaging through 'insanely great' designed products.<br /><br />Genius has its quirks.  Even the cover art of the book is reminiscent of the now famous Jobs unblinking stare.  As others recounted both personal and professional behaviors of the man, my first thought was that Jobs was highly functional while residing at some point in the Asperger's spectrum.  I'm likely wrong but symptomatically there seems to be a correlation.<br /><br />While not particularly philanthropic, it's difficult to overstate the significance Jobs has had on transforming the way in which humanity accesses so much of its daily content.  He and his companies (Apple, Pixar, etc.) reconfigured entire industries (i.e. music, digital animation, etc.) and the mechanisms by which we all consume this array of media.<br /><br />The book provides telling insights by those closest to Jobs both personally and professionally.  The writer certainly felt the pull of time as Jobs approached the end of his life as the book was being written.  'Steve Jobs - The Biography' serves as an epistle of sorts.  From Jobs perspective he acknowledged wanting to have something in writing so his children knew the value of his work - time that took him away from his family.  Too, the irony exists that for such a controlling personality, he chose not to exert any editorial sway over the narrative.by those closest to Jobs both personally and professionally. By virtue of being classic, that often means that readers need to tolerate a certain degree of cliche, which is true here, but Colfer also uses many original twists to keep this tale fresh. This is a story of twins Alex and Conner Bailey (twins are ever-popular in kids' lit!) and their accidental journey to a fairy tale world when they fall into a book of fairy tales given to them by their Grandma. Colfer shines when describing how important fairy tales are to Alex, and readers will be invested in why she specifically needs that world so badly. In the world Alex and Conner are from, their dad has died and their mom had to move them into a shabbier home in order to make ends meet, so the fairy tale realm is seen as a true escape.<br /><br />However, it's also dangerous, and they quickly realize the necessity of getting back home. The plot revolves around the twins' efforts to collect the items they need for the Wishing Spell, which should help them achieve their goal. Like in the Wizard of Oz, the Wishing Spell proves to be less important towards this end than they first thought, but during their journey, they experience a great deal of the fairy tale world, traveling to kingdoms run by Queen Cinderella, Queen Rapunzel, Queen Red Riding Hood, Queen Sleeping Beauty, and Queen Snow White, along with the Dwarf Forests, the Troll and Goblin Territory, and the Fairy Kingdom. The Happily Ever After Assembly is parallel to the United Nations, helping make laws and settle conflicts.<br /><br />All of the characters are classic archetypes, without the quirks of individual creations like some fantasy novels. They stay pretty true-to-form, with the exception of Queen Red Riding Hood, who is hilariously vain and selfish, and the Evil Queen, whose reason for being evil is eventually explored in depth in a successful passage. While the narrative is fairly predictable, most children will not have read enough fantasy for this to be an issue, and I think that they themselves will enjoy this book. That being said, right from the beginning it was very obvious who wrote the journal that guided them to the Wishing Spell.<br /><br />There were some stretches in terms of plot points - on occasion easier actions were available, yet the characters involved did not take them, or else what they did do seemed unlikely to actually succeed outside of a story. For example, when they're on the lookout for Red Riding Hood's original basket and are shown to the basket room by a handmaiden, they could have just asked the handmaiden which was the original, rather than searching through hundreds of baskets.<br /><br />Colfer's story is excellent at illustrating how different ways of thinking can be useful in real life situations. Conner feels inept within the context of a classroom, but his outside-the-box perspective allows him to cross a stream in the fairy tale world without waiting around to solve the bridge troll's riddle, and he climbs over the wall of a kindgom instead of spending hours looking for the entrance. He thinks quickly on his feet, and this saves them from many a jam. Alex's compassion, intelligence, and knowledge of fairy tales also plays a role in their quest. She is a character many young girls will be able to relate to - ostracized at school for being smart, but very lonely. She considers the fairy tale characters her best friends.<br /><br />Colfer pairs Jack and the Beanstalk with Goldilocks, making a convincing argument since they are both outdoor adventurers. Portraying Queen Red Riding Hood as horribly jealous because she wants to marry Jack is a fun twist that I think kids will get a kick out of, especially during a climactic point when Goldilocks and Red Riding Hood essentially have a catfight. Trollbella was hideously cute and she is another character that will resonate with children.<br /><br />There were a few plot twists towards the endthat I did not see coming (even if in retrospect, I should have), and this bodes well for child readers. The Land of Stories: The Wishing Spell is very much in line with classic fantasy and fairy tales from earlier time periods, rather than modern fantasy, so I would recommend this book to children who enjoyed The Wizard of Oz, Alice in Wonderland, Peter Pan, etc.that I did not see coming (even if in retrospect, I should have), and this bodes well for child readers. The Land of Stories: The Wishing Spell is very much in line with classic fantasy and fairy tales from earlier time periods, rather than modern fantasy, so I would recommend this book to children who enjoyed The Wizard of Oz, Alice in Wonderland, Peter Pan, etc.

Clips hold the device firmly in place.<br />-- Adds minimal bulk or weight to the device. There is barely any gap around the Fire, so you're only adding about a millimeter, on all three dimensions, by adding this cover.<br />-- Has an elastic strap to ensure the cover stays closed. This is a nice feature that my old Kindle Keyboard's lighted case had, and I prefer an elastic closure, if possible.<br />-- Great feel, smooth leather feels great in the hand. Inner liner is fuzzy and will protect the screen well. Love that I can get it in red, for the \\"fire\\" aspect of the Fire HD's name. :)<br />-- Stands up on its own in landscape mode (though see my concern, below).<br /><br />CONS<br />-- Case is really stiff for stand-ups; I'm sure this will work itself out, in time, and I rarely use the stand-up feature anyways.<br />-- Major issue with how you orient the Fire HD - camera-right or camera-left. Depending on orientation, you'll have partial functionality either way.<br /><br />ORIENTATION ISSUE<br />-- The issue is that, basically, you have two installation options:<br /><br />1) Camera-to-the-right: This puts the HDMI/USB on the left, and power/headphone/volume on the bottom. This allows the camera to be \\"up\\" when you stand the unit up in the case and allows for the auto-sleep to work. However, it prohibits the Fire HD from charging or displaying on HDMI when the case is closed or deployed in \\"stand mode,\\" and prohibits use of non-Bluetooth headphones while propped on lap in portrait orientation.<br />2) Camera-to-the-left: This, I believe, is the desired installation option (and is, based on Amazon's case). However, when standing the case up, it results in the camera being on the bottom, and auto-sleep will not work.<br /><br />If you look at the Amazon-branded case, it specifically uses the orientation I, personally, prefer - Camera-to-the-left. They even have special cutouts for the USB and HDMI on the right. So I believe this is the desired installation method. Yet, in this orientation, the Marware case cannot auto-sleep or stand on its own and have the camera on top.<br /><br />All in all, I recommend this case for Fire HD 7\\" users. The price is much more reasonable than the stock Amazon-branded case, which I think is absolutely horrifically priced. I fail to understand how Amazon can, in good conscience, sell a technological marvel that is the Fire HD for an obscenely-low $199, and then charge 25% of the cost for a case! All of their cases are like this, which is ridiculous. Maybe they count on it, and make some of the money up on the case? :)<br /><br />---UPDATED 17-Sep-2012---<br />Updated the above due to comments on how the case does, indeed, sleep the unit if you have it in the \\"wrong\\" orientation. Thanks to Leticia for making me double check this! Lowered stars to 4 due to the no-win situation this case presents, though it's still an excellent-feeling case and a good value.rientation I, personally, prefer - Camera-to-the-left. B0083V2VW8|4.0|Earlier this year I bought, from Amazon UK, the Universal Pictures 2011 Blu-ray of Quadrophenia - which is a genuine full 1080p transfer from film, and with almost exactly the same extra features as for their 2009 \\"2 Disc Special Edition\\" DVD. That Blu-ray provides an amazing amount of extra detail over what the DVD produced, and the audio was noticeably (but slightly) better than the DVD version (which sounded excellent). I was surprised to find a Criterion edition announced several months later, and I ordered it (from Amazon USA) for the several different extra features, and I hoped that the video and audio might even be a touch better.<br /><br />I've compared the two Blu-ray editions in an A-B test, and I believe that the Universal Blu-ray has significantly better detail. For some strange reason The Criterion edition is slightly and annoyingly zoomed, cutting away details which the Universal edition proves were clearly meant to have been seen in the widescreen frame. The Criterion video is much brighter (which is not necessarily an improvement) - and although it is slightly fuzzy, it is definitely more pristine (the Universal has a small number of barely noticeable glitches, which are not visible on the Criterion).<br /><br />I haven't heard the Criterion commentary track yet (director Franc Roddam and cinematographer Brian Tufano), but I certainly enjoyed the Universal one with Franc Roddam and actor Phil Daniels.<br /><br />The audio for both Blu-ray editions is excellent - both for the restored original stereo and for each edition's sometimes starkly different 5.1 surround options (with the Criterion having the superior edge). The answer is to ideally own both editions to enjoy the best of each. However in the UK and Australia the \\"All Regions\\" Universal edition is very cheap, and fans ought to be quite content with it - especially because the much more expensive Criterion edition is Region A locked.<br /><br />I'm very happy that one of my top five favouritefilms has been restored and preserved for future appreciation in not just one, but two excellent quality high definition editions.

0393343405|5.0|Whatever you do, don't let the pedants, jealous academics, and Christian apologists keep you from enjoying this wonderful book. Maybe the thesis is a bit overstated, but there is so much truth to discover in this ancient poem and the author's explanation of it's rediscovery. I am sure that the experts could rightfully quibble about the earthshaking impact of the poem, but the ideas are timeless and even more relevant in a world becoming more secular. The fact is that religious orthodoxy has had a negative influence on human progress while promising the myth of an afterlife. The poem reaffirms that the purpose of life is life itself, and that we need to learn to appreciate the time we have here because that's all there is. Religion celebrates an angry demanding capricious deity interpreted by intermediaries who provide the rules to unlock the mystery. For most of us, it just doesn't work. Lucretius understood.

0061692042|5.0|If you are looking for a fast paced narrative, full of suspense and robbers on the run (r.o.r. - okay, just made that up), then look elsewhere.<br />But if you know Ford's elegant, stripped down prose and amazing ability to capture the intricacies of human beings better than any other writer alive (stolen quote form Globe and Mail), and you have the time to immerse yourself in a slow exploration... then this book needs 6 stars, or maybe more.<br /><br />I know it won't be for everyone - and especially the dismaying amount of readers that want a narrative to drive forward - but this book will ache in my chest long after I have read it. I don't need to give away much in the plot, really there isn't much plot. In 1960, Dell Parson's parents rob a bank, he and his twin sister are set adrift. Berner, his sister, runs to California, Dell is spirited away, pre-911, to cross the border into Canada, and \\"hide-out\\" in southwestern Saskatchewan. (Right around where I was born actually). It really isn't a hiding out, but a crossing of a physical border, that parallels the metaphorical border Dell is crossing in his life.<br /><br />Sure it is a coming of age of sorts - for me it resonates best with Ford's short stories (Rock Springs) and the novella Wildlife, which also has a young person at its centre, and a shares the setting of Great Falls, Montana. It has less in common with the Bascombe books (Independence Day etc., which I have not liked as much.)<br />But it is much more than a sixteen year old crossing into adulthood. A melancholy pervades the book, at times it's almost too sad to read, as in when the siblings visit their parents in jail. Ford gets inside people, and even if you can't imagine what Dell's life would be like (ie: have never had a parent commit a criminal act and go to jail), you will recognize yourself in there, you will recognize humanity in there.<br /><br />Dell tells this story as a 66-year old man (though, this only becomes evident in scant ways, andthe voice is a teenager's, one wise beyond his years). And I can't help but think Ford is reflecting on his life through this book - he is returning to a setting from early in his career, and he is thinking about the things that led up to his life now (I think he is almost 70). As I've said, this is a sad and quiet book - but in the best sort of Wim Wenders way. I do think it will make the Pulitzer and National Book lists.<br /><br />If I have one criticism of the book, it is the use of the cliffhanger type endings in some of the chapters. As in, \\"and he knew he would never see again\\", or \\"later when he found the man dead in the room\\" (these are paraphrase samples, trying not to spoil anything - but hell, right from the beginning line you know there is a robbery and a murder). I wonder if editors told Ford, \\"You know it's beautiful and all that, but can you crank up the tension, just a bit?\\" These bits seem out of place. And I want to say, shut up unnamed editor, I am just fine with the pace. I have seen Ford in interviews, including the Colbert Report (!), himself reminding the interviewer that the book is also about robbery and murders, as if to say, \\"hey, it's not like that literary stuff that people don't read anymore.\\" Again, I say, Richard, shhh. It's fine. It's more than fine.<br /><br />I know this is a book I will read again. The controlled and elegant prose needs to be studied. The mood is not something I look forward to, but the feelings, and even the truth, that it evokes create something that I find in classic novels: at the end, I am changed.<br /><br />At this point in my life (49), I think a lot of my Saskatchewan upbringing. Incidentally, my father was a goose hunter, also born right around the setting of the novel, and these sections of the book are crystalline in their imagery. And I think of borders, what it means to cross over them, and to never return.<br />My favorite quote in the book is the narrator quoting Ruskin,<br />\\"Composition is thearrangement of unequal things.\\"<br /><br />This is what Ford does. He takes these unequal things in our lives, and he puts them into stories that tell us who we are.

145162607X|4.0|\\"I think of Elizabeth Woodville, who has been the bane of my days and the murderer of my sister... \\"I have a greater claim to the throne than her.  I shall be, as I was supposed to be, Queen Anne of England.\\"<br /><br />This is the next installment in Philippa Gregory's covering of the War of the Roses. As always it is really well researched, and mixes just enough fact with fiction to be a really entertaining read.<br /><br />This book follows the life of Anne Neville: daughter to the Kingmaker the Earl of Warwick, wife to a rebellious prince, duchess to the brother of a King, and eventually Queen of England. Anne is not a very prominent figure in history, however Gregory manages to pull enough together of her life to make her past come alive. While a lot of this book is probably speculation, and is most likely not historically accurate as to the level of participation Anne had in these events, it does give a pretty accurate picture overall of what was happening during this time.<br /><br />If you haven't read any of the other books in this series (The White Queen, The Red Queen, Lady of the Rivers) you can still pick this book up and read it as a standalone novel. It will read just like any other Philippa Gregory novel in that sense. There are a couple allusions to the previous novels she makes but they are so small that only someone who has read all the books will catch on to them.<br /><br />For those who have been following the series, I think you will find this book a very interesting addition. In most of the novels up to now we have seen Elizabeth Woodville as a good character, or at the very least pretty neutral. This book decidedly makes her a villain. From Anne's point of view Elizabeth and the York court become centers of intrigue and plot. Every story we have read up until now gets turned on it's head and examined from the other side. I found I quite enjoyed it even if there were some events that were repetitive from other books.<br /><br />Overall I would suggest checking out this series. It is by far better than the Tudor series in my opinion, and provides a rich story surrounding the turmoil of England during this series of wars. I'm eagerly awaiting the release of the White Princess to finish out the Plantagenet writing for these amazing and influential women of history.<br /><br />This review also seen on the Caffeinated Booker.

B004H1V5RU|4.0|This is a rather simple puzzle game, in which the goal is to roll a small steel ball along a series of tracks and obstacles from a starting point to the finish line.  There are 5 checkpoints along the way, which you can enjoy independently as slightly shorter puzzles.  While it may look like a maze, it's really not very maze-like, in that there is really only a single track to follow, with each platform numbered in the order in which it is meant to be traversed.  The challenge is in navigating the platforms themselves, with their numerous obstacles, pitfalls, and precarious drops.<br /><br />If you've used the Perplexus Maze and are ready to move up in difficulty, this is the next level.  This puzzle is substantially more difficult than the easier incarnations (the Perplexus Maze and Perplexus Rookie).  I question the manufacturer's age recommendation of ages 6-12 on this product, I personally would qualify it as for ages 10-Adult.  While this product is likely perfectly safe and usable by a 6yr-old, I question that they would have the stamina and motivation to play with and solve the puzzle as it's intended to be solved.  Every adult that I've showed this to, however, can't resist picking it up and giving it a try (I have yet to observe someone succeed in navigating the puzzle from start to finish).  For anyone really, I would highly recommend starting with one of the easier models (Perplexus Maze or Rookie), and move up to this one once you're ready for a greater challenge.  Most adults will find the Perplexus Maze equally engaging, while not quite as frustrating to solve.  While more challenging, this version is equally addictive.

Not only can you use the largest of heatsinks available, but you can also change to a complicated radiator setup with no modification. The fact that the top can support 240mm with room to spare for 140mm radiators on the back and bottom of makes this case very flexible in regards to cooling options.<br /><br />The one sad thing is the front intake. The front fan mounts are \\"snap in\\" plastic clips with peg mounts to keep down vibration and add stability. While this setup is convenient for installation purposes, it limits fans to box style housing. Although many quality fans fit (I'm enjoying 2 Yate-Loons) it does limit things as some of the highest rated 140mm fans use rounded frames that cannot be held in by the clips. This arrangement also precludes using radiators on the front without major modifications to the case.<br /><br />The fan filters provided are lightweight and do a good job of filtering the larger dust particles. I don't get dust bunnies, but I do get a thin coating of dust; however it is easy to remove with compressed air. The top and bottom filters are removeable. Unfortunately, if you remove the top filter, you expose the fan support grills underneath and the case definitely looks as if it is \\"missing something\\". (Yes that also means that the top fans must deal with both a filter and a grill!) The front fan filter also has a honeycomb grill and a filter, but the grill is the front protection that you see on the outside of the case.<br /><br />Fractal has designed a wonderful case that targets high-end users with a mid-range cost. B008B0QH5S|3.0|I've been a PSB fan since I was a teenager in the early 90s, and have ever album, single, b-side, video, etc that they have released. They are one of only three artists in my music collection where I am an absolute completist, so I bought this album sight unseen, day one purchase, instabuy.<br /><br />Now that I'm having some time to sit down and give it a good listen, I have to admit I am disappointed. For me, the reason why Neil & Chris have managed to keep my attention all these years is that they make good, incredibly catchy electronic inspired pop music. Now when you think about that statement, you have to realize that that is incredibly difficult to do. There is a ton of \\"pop\\" music out there, and an enormous plethora of \\"electronic\\" music, but PSB have always managed to rise above the chaff and create meaningful lyrics, introspective phrases and passages, amidst a grand stage of uptempo beats mingled with even catchier down-tempo songs.<br /><br />My first bump in the road with the Pettos has to be with the album Release, put out around 10 years ago. Nary an electronic beat to be heard amidst what was more or less an acoustic album. I gave it many tries, I really did, and finally decided that this just was not their sound. Now, unfortunately, we have what I am terming the second Release album - Elysium. I know I know, bands have to \\"evolve\\", give it some time, you'll love it, it will grow on you.<br /><br />Well... perhaps. But upon first listening, I am once again as disappointed as I was those many years ago. Ballad after god awful ballad fills this disc, with a couple of catchier up tempo pieces to fill in the gaps between the absolute boredom of Inivisible, Hold On, Give It A Go (really, can we please GO, instead of sitting in the easy chair!?!?)<br /><br />I honestly do not believe Chris Lowe had much input on this album - his fiery beats and melodies are nowhere to be found here, and even Neil is trying too hard to bring life to such droll lyrics, thatI question how much control he really had over the writing as well. I know in the music biz it is common for artists to move from producer to producer on each album, so that their sound doesn't become too stale, but in this case, I think PSB have found someone who utterly ruined their sound.<br /><br />I will give this album a few more listens, and may shelve it for several months before another go, but I'm not too hopeful that time will cure what ails this one. Hopefully the B-sides on the first single are as good as I've read... maybe there is hope still for just one more barn stormer album? Come on Neil & Chris, I know you can \\"Give It A Go\\"!<br /><br />Update 09/15/12<br /><br />Second listen through, and I regret having used the word \\"god awful\\" above to refer to much of the slower pieces on the album. I think the more appropriate description is tranquil. Clearly the boys have set out on a more chill vibe this go-around, but the second time through I didn't find it nearly as offensive, as I knew what to expect.<br /><br />As for the comparisons to the reaction that Behaviour got when it was released - I listened to that album straight through immediately after Elysium, and I believe the comparisons are not fair. Behaviour to me is a much more detailed album, filled with nuances and messages that take many listenings to get. Many of the songs themselves are crafted much more intricately than most of the ones on Elysium. And for me, nobody, not even the boys, will ever be able to top Being Boring (my personal favorite song of all time).<br /><br />So - I'm upping my rating to 3 stars. Unlike a lot of people, I actually really like the track Winner, but that may be because I absolutely love watching the Summer Olympics every 4 years. Requiem in Denim and Leopardskin is also another song that stands out - not because I think it's particularly catchy, but because it vaguely reminds me of Your Funny Uncle. It's almost got a kind of lounge feel to it too, I dunno, it's an interesting track.<br /><br />Anyways... I've said enough.

0307594165|4.0|Home is lean novel (a novella really) that tells the story of two siblings who return home to Lotus, Georgia, a place that holds few fond memories for them, and the redemption they find there.  I've read a number of novels by Morrison, who I consider to be one of the most talented authors to ever put pen to paper, and I think most people will applaud Home while at the same time be left feeling a little let down - as if there should be something more substantial to it.  The novel is satisfying in its own modest way, but it's not nearly as complex or ambitious as some of Morrison's greatest works.  It certainly isn't as haunting as Beloved was.<br /><br />The story: Frank Money is back from the Korean War and is wandering aimlessly, with no plans to return to his home town until he receives word that his sister is dying.  Frank is dealing with the horrors of war and has a guilty secret he is keeping not only from the reader but himself.  Frank rescues his sister from a doctor whose experiments have nearly killed her and the siblings return home to Lotus, Georgia.<br /><br />The prose in this novel is leaner, and arguably not as poetic, as other novels by the author but it is still beautifully poignant at times.  I enjoyed this novel.  Despite its brevity it feels complete even if it isn't as powerful as Beloved or as lyrical as Song of Solomon.

I have never written a negative review of anything in my life, but the hype of this book is so inexplicably HUGE that I feel compelled to say something. Reading The Name of the Wind was not just a waste of time, it made me feel bitter and angry that such popularity and acclaim could be ascribed to something that seemed to me to be so poorly executed.<br /><br />I could not get past page 200, in fact, and struggle to comprehend how people can think this book is well-written or the characters at all believable. It is trite and contrived. So much of the story is clichÃ©, contradictory, or just plain silly that I found myself rolling my eyes or grunting to myself and shaking my head in disbelief at just about every page.<br /><br />I apologize if you enjoyed it. I admit that sometimes I find a story so interesting that I can overlook some of this stuff myself, but for whatever reason, I just couldn't take it in The Name of the Wind. I had to cry Uncle and do what I so very rarely do: give up on a book.J.K. Rowling and George R. R. Martin to name just a few.<br /><br />There are just too much great literature and fantasy books out there to waste time on stuff like this. And this is coming from a person who still reads a fair number of \\"candy\\" sort of fantasy and thriller type books. I just wish people would be a little more discenring and appreciate the troves of amazing books that are available, thousands of which are free in the public domain! But in the end all that really matters is that you enjoy what you read, and if you aren't picky, you may love this one.

B001KNUUTM|5.0|This collection is by far one of the best recordings of the WTC available today.  I have listened to many recordings including those of Glenn Gould, Angela Hewitt, Andras Schiff, and Till Felner, and have to say this is my favorite.  Till Felner's recording of the first book comes in at a close second and might even have tied for first if he had recorded both books of the WTC instead of just book one.<br />  At times, Feltsman employs a deatched touch that results in a quasi-Gouldian tone.  However, unlike Gould, Feltsman varies his articulation (I love Gould's spikey, rhythm-driven approach, but it is not always appropriate). Schiff and Hewitt are too \\"proper\\" for my taste.  While they are creative in their phrasing, they are not as creative in their articulation. Schiff has a beautiful legato, but EVERY prelude and Fugue is legato. Felner suffers from the same over-romantic touch. If you are looking for an interpretation as varied in style and ingenuity as the pieces themselves, this is the set for you!  It can be tiresome to listen to so much polyphonic music in one sitting, but Feltsman's diverse approach gives each prelude and fugue a distinctive character eliminating the fatigue experienced whilst listening to other performances.  I second the other reviewer's remarks about sound quality, Feltsman's recording engineers did a superb job.

B004J6YO12|4.0|This 1970s/early 1980s British band, originating in Birmingham, England, was decidedly different from most in that era in that their concerts and records in their early years employed a string section in an overall gathering of eight members. While always having much more commercial success in their home country, with a couple of albums reaching # 1 on their charts, in North America none of their material ever reached those heights. Still, from late 1972 to 1986 they did put 27 singles onto the Billboard Pop Hot 100/Bubble Under charts along with 10 crossovers to the Adult Contemporary (AC) listings.<br /><br />This album, which was released in 1979 on Jet FZ 35769, an independent U.K. label launched by Don Arden and distributed in North America through CBS, was their first U.K. # 1, and in the U.S. it reached # 5 on Billboard's Top 200.  It also produced four U.S. hit singles, the first being Shine A Little Love, a # 8 Hot 100/# 40 AC in May/June 1979 on Jet 5057 b/w Jungle. Later that summer Don't Bring Me Down became their best U.S. Hot 100 single when it reached # 4 in late August/early September on Jet 5060 b/w Dreaming Of 4000. In late October/early November, Confusion topped out at # 37 Hot 100/# 41 AC on Jet 5064 b/w Poker, and they closed that year out with the # 39 Hot 100 Last Train To London on Jet 5067 b/w Down Home Town. None of the B-sides were part of the album.<br /><br />Those four sides also became hit singles in the U.K. along with another track, The Diary Of Horace Wimp, with most of them reflecting the burgeoning Disco sound. Which was probably the main reason why the lineup did not include their string section of violinist Mik Kaminski and cellists Hugh McDowell and Melvyn Gale, who had been part of their first 15 U.S. hits. However, in the music videos created to promote the album in the U.K., that trio did participate. The main band consisted of Jeff Lynne on vocals, guitar, piano and synthesizer, bass guitarist Kelly Groucutt (also vocals), pianist Richard Tandy (also synthesizer, clavinet and electric piano), and drummer/percussionist Bev Bevan. The backing orchestra was conducted by Louis Clark.<br /><br />A bit of trivia associated with the album sleeve is the photo on the back cover depicting a fierce-looking Middle-East palace guard drawing his scimitar. That, in one of his earliest professional jobs, is none other than Brad Garrett, perhaps now best-remembered as the 6' 8.5\\" tall cop brother Robert of Ray Romano's character in the hit sitcom Everybody Loves Raymond!<br /><br />The tracks (all written by Jeff Lynne), are:<br />Side One<br />1. 0007477325|4.0|\\"One percent of people will always be honest and never steal. Another one percent will always be dishonest and always try to pick your lock and steal your television. And the rest will be honest as long as the conditions are right.\\" ~An anonymous locksmith<br /><br />When I was a using addict, I lied so much that it became second nature. I told big lies, small lies, dirty lies, barefaced lies, white lies, and black ones too. My motto was, \\"Deny everything.\\" Even when the deconstruction of my fabrication was readily apparent and the evidence of my malfeasance displayed before me...I lied.<br /><br />  I didn't realize that this particular shortcoming, one among many, was yet another manifestation of my ugly addiction. I just assumed that I was a liar, always was, and always will be, one more shameful label branded upon my less than reputable character. So I was relieved to discover that that's not who I truly am. You see, addiction cannot survive without dishonesty. Honesty is to addiction what water is to fire, what light is to darkness. The two cannot co-exist. And that would explain why the topic at my first recovery meeting was...you guessed it - honesty.<br /><br />  So that's why I would waste my oh-so-precious time reading a book titled \\"The (Honest) Truth About Dishonesty\\". It's written by Dan Ariely who is a professor of psychology and behavioral economics at Duke University. He conducted many experiments to discover what motivates a person to cheat as well as what keeps them honest. He also discovered a couple of things that, counter-intuitively, have no effect on honesty.<br /><br />  So what increases dishonesty? Conflicts of interest, watching others behave dishonestly, the ability to rationalize our dishonesty, and, surprisingly, creativity, all seem to increase dishonesty. What decreases it? Taking pledges before the temptation, moral reminders such as honor codes or the ten commandments, also presented right before the temptation, and supervision (obviously), decreased cheating. And what had no effect?<br /><br />  Contrary to what you might expect, Ariely found that the amount of money to be gained and the probability of getting caught had no effect on whether a person would lie cheat or steal. And he found that most people, at least the people who participated in his experiments, will cheat a tiny bit, up to the level that allows them to retain their self-image as a reasonably honest individual.<br /><br />  While this book didn't exactly deal with the level of dishonesty that accompanies addiction, I still found it very interesting, chock-full of humorous anecdotes and Ariely's witty observations. I learned a little more about human nature, which is a fascinating subject to me, and you can bet that I'll be more aware of the forces at play the next time I'm tempted to lie, cheat, or steal.<br /><br />Review Written by David Allan Reeves<br />Author of \\"Running Away From Me\\"iously), decreased cheating. B004ZZS4CC|5.0|I didn't know quite what to expect of this, but in the end found it very moving. Jaycee Dugard chooses to tell her story in her own voice - she did not use a ghostwriter. While this means that the writing is simplistic at times (remember she only received formal education through 5th grade), it is straight from the heart. Some sections read like a journal (and in fact she includes some passages from a journal she kept while in captivity), others read more like a therapy session, and some are very basic descriptions of her day-to-day life in captivity. Because it is her telling her own story, it is focused on what she remembers. In 'Reflections' sections she sometimes shares information that she learned later, but this book doesn't try to relay the 'whole' story, in terms of filling in what her mother and authorities were doing to find her, or any of that. It has a very insular feeling, which it seems to me mirrors exactly the isolated life that she was forced to live for so long.<br /><br />The early chapters are difficult to read, because she does recount the horrible rapes and abuse she was subjected too. Once her daughters are born, the story shifts to their bizarre day to day life. Animals were a huge part of what kept her going, and so she shares many animal stories (including the ways that these were frequently taken from her, another method of manipulation.) Once she is discovered and reunited with her family, she shares just a few stories from her unique therapy, which included horses since animals were such a big way she and her daughters related to the world. 18 years is a lot to cover, and she can only remember so much, so there are gaps. Certainly I found myself wanting more information in places, so that I could try and understand this horrific crime. But it can't really be understood, and the purpose of this book isn't to try and do so. It is Jaycee telling her own story, and I respect her so much for doing so.<br /><br />I think complaints about the writing, the graphicness, the 'boring' details re: her life and animals are all unfair. She is sharing her experience to the best of her abilities, and in that sense, this is one of the most authentic-feeling memoirs I have ever read. While I found myself wanting more, and almost went online to research her situation more, I decided not to, because I don't want to feed the hunger for information about her. She and her daughters deserve to live the rest of their lives in privacy and safety, away from the public eye. I wish that for them, and I hope the proceeds from this book will help them to do so.riting, the graphicness, the 'boring' details re: her life and animals are all unfair. This young adult novel not only captivates youthful minds through mystery and fantasy, but it also intrigues the older reader by exploring the exciting history of film. 1400069289|3.0|As most reviews say, both entertaining and instructive, but some caveats are in order. Mr. Duhigg writes a nice journalistic style (yes, that is a compliment), but has a tendency to elaborate only one side of some the stories he tells to illustrate contemporary ideas about habit, thus oversimplifying for effect.<br /><br />What do I mean?  A couple of specific examples: Paul O'Neill gets superlatives for transforming the \\"keystone habit\\" of safety at Alcoa, including firing an otherwise excellent Brazil manager who corrected but failed to promptly report a safety issue; however,O'Neill's subsequent inability to transfer his method to his tenure as Treasury Secretary is shrugged off in a footnote as political differences with Bush Administration.  Well, maybe so, but could it be that Mr. O'Neil misidentified and/or misapplied the appropriate keystone habit needed for a different job?  Or possibly that he didn't appreciate that one of the necessary habits for being effective as a cabinet officer may be to criticize internally, but then get on board with the boss's keystone policy (the mistake that got the Brazil manager fired)?  Again, in Duhigg's chapter about the opportunity presented by a crisis to change old routines, Duhigg argues that crises disrupt the status quo (not a surprise), but then goes further to commend use of a crisis (by manipulation, if necessary) to further one's agenda to promulgate unrelated policies or, arguably, implement old routines of failed government. I suppose his quoting of Rahm Emmanuel that \\"a crisis should never be wasted\\" shows the dangers of trying to be contemporary when making larger points!  Unfortunately, crises may be used to implement bad changes as well as good, and you can get Hitler as easily as Roosevelt, a drunkard as easily as a reformed alcoholic: So maybe a less unconditional endorsement of using (especially manipulating) crises as means to generate new routines (in government, business or personal lives) should comewith more caveats.<br /><br />Despite its lapses into self-help book (or presumably intention, given the how-to-change-habits Appendix), Mr. Duhigg has written an informative report on the current science and understanding of habit. I don't hesitate in joining others in recommending it as an easy-to-read (again, this is a compliment) and up-to-date discussion of what we know about the power of habit. If the book was only a bit more careful in its advocacy of habit manipulation, four stars would be deserved instead of three.
